class="mantine-Grid-col mantine-3816o2"><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-i7qrnu" id="post" data-component="post-content"><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p><strong>Introduction</strong></p><p>We discovered an installer downloaded from the official website of <a href="http://monpass.mn/"><code>MonPass</code></a>, a major certification authority (CA) in Mongolia in East Asia that was backdoored with Cobalt Strike binaries. We immediately notified MonPass on 22 April 2021 of our findings and encouraged them to address their compromised server and notify those who downloaded the backdoored client.</p><p>We have confirmed with MonPass that they have taken steps to address these issues and are now presenting our analysis.</p><p>Our analysis beginning in April 2021 indicates that a public web server hosted by MonPass was breached potentially eight separate times: we found eight different webshells and backdoors on this server. We also found that the MonPass client available for download from 8 February 2021 until 3 March 2021 was backdoored.&nbsp;</p><p>This research provides analysis of relevant backdoored installers and other samples that we found occurring in the wild. Also during our investigation we observed relevant <a href="https://hello.global.ntt/-/media/ntt/global/insights/white-papers/the-operations-of-winnti-group.pdf">research from NTT Ltd</a> so some technical details or IoCs may overlap.</p><p>All the samples are highly similar and share the same pdb path:</p><p><code>C:\Users\test\Desktop\fishmaster\x64\Release\fishmaster.pdb</code> and the string: <code>Bidenhappyhappyhappy</code>.</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200_19.png.webp?itok=73Vy-6zv 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200_19.png.webp?itok=YFzTrIXp 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200_19.png.webp?itok=qXWJ6y0b 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200_19.png.webp?itok=IUJ0f_UT 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200_19.png.webp?itok=Fc2Cd5n0 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200_19.png.webp?itok=y19zMEdL 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img alt="Figure 1: Pdb path and specific string" width="300" height="300" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="mantine-q24tb8" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:100%" srcSet="/blog/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gendigital.com%2Fblog%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_paragraph_image_small%2Fpublic%2F2024-06%2Fpasted%2520image%25200_19.png.webp%3Fitok%3DFc2Cd5n0&amp;w=384&amp;q=75 1x, /blog/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gendigital.com%2Fblog%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_paragraph_image_small%2Fpublic%2F2024-06%2Fpasted%2520image%25200_19.png.webp%3Fitok%3DFc2Cd5n0&amp;w=640&amp;q=75 2x" src="/blog/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gendigital.com%2Fblog%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_paragraph_image_small%2Fpublic%2F2024-06%2Fpasted%2520image%25200_19.png.webp%3Fitok%3DFc2Cd5n0&amp;w=640&amp;q=75"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 1: Pdb path and specific string</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p><strong>Technical details</strong></p><p>The malicious installer is an unsigned PE file. It starts by downloading the legitimate version of the installer from the MonPass official website. This legitimate version is dropped to the <code>C:\Users\Public\</code> folder and executed under a new process. This guarantees that the installer behaves as expected, meaning that a regular user is unlikely to notice anything suspicious.</p><p>Additional similar installers were also found in the wild, with SHA256 hashes:&nbsp; <code>e2596f015378234d9308549f08bcdca8eadbf69e488355cddc9c2425f77b7535 </code>and&nbsp;<code>f21a9c69bfca6f0633ba1e669e5cf86bd8fc55b2529cd9</code></p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_8.png.webp?itok=_Jd8MnBk 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_8.png.webp?itok=vSoYqWvj 2x" media="(min-width: 1020px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_8.png.webp?itok=SHBWsJ6M 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_8.png.webp?itok=mAk3Z8tM 2x" media="(min-width: 720px)"/><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_8.png.webp?itok=t5FtEeGu 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2024-06/pasted%20image%200-1_8.png.webp?itok=NsFdeWX4 2x" media="(min-width: 420px)"/><img alt="Figure 2: This image is not as innocent as it may seem." width="300" height="300" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" class="mantine-q24tb8" style="color:transparent;width:100%;height:100%" srcSet="/blog/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gendigital.com%2Fblog%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_paragraph_image_small%2Fpublic%2F2024-06%2Fpasted%2520image%25200-1_8.png.webp%3Fitok%3Dt5FtEeGu&amp;w=384&amp;q=75 1x, /blog/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gendigital.com%2Fblog%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_paragraph_image_small%2Fpublic%2F2024-06%2Fpasted%2520image%25200-1_8.png.webp%3Fitok%3Dt5FtEeGu&amp;w=640&amp;q=75 2x" src="/blog/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gendigital.com%2Fblog%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_paragraph_image_small%2Fpublic%2F2024-06%2Fpasted%2520image%25200-1_8.png.webp%3Fitok%3Dt5FtEeGu&amp;w=640&amp;q=75"/></picture><div class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 2: This image is not as innocent as it may seem.</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>The attackers decided to use steganography to transfer shellcode to their victims. On execution, the malware downloads a bitmap image file from http://download.google-images[.]ml:8880/download/37.bmp as shown in <code>figure 2</code>.&nbsp;</p><p>The download is performed slightly unusually in two HTTP requests. The first request uses the HEAD method to retrieve the Content-Length, followed by a second GET request to actually download the image. After the picture is downloaded, the malware extracts the encrypted payload as follows. The hidden data is expected to be up to 0x76C bytes. Starting with the 3rd byte in image data it copies each 4th byte. The resulting data represents an ASCII string of hexadecimal characters which is later decoded into their respective binary values. These bytes are then XOR decrypted using the hardcoded key <code>miat_mg</code>, resulting in a Cobalt-Strike beacon.</p><p>We have seen multiple versions of this backdoored installer, each with slightly modified decryptors.&nbsp;</p><p>In version (<code>f21a9c69bfca6f0633ba1e669e5cf86bd8fc55b2529cd9b064ff9e2e129525e8</code>) the XOR decryption was stripped.<br><br>In the version (<code>e2596f015378234d9308549f08bcdca8eadbf69e488355cddc9c2425f77b7535</code>) basic anti-analysis tricks were stripped. In Figure 3, you can see different time stamps and the same rich headers.</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 4: Rich header.</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p>In the backdoored installer we also observed some basic anti-analysis techniques used in an attempt to avoid detection. In particular, we observed checks for the number of processors using the <code><em>GetSystemInfo</em></code> function, the amount of physical memory using the <code><em>GlobalMemoryStatusEx</em></code> function and the disk capacity using the <code>IOCTL_DISK_GET_DRIVE_GEOMETRY</code> IOCTL call. If any of the obtained values are suspiciously low, the malware terminates immediately.</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture id="the-picture"><source srcSet="https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2024-06/monpass_1.png.webp?itok=QzVze5y2 1x, infected installers found in our customers. The sample contained anti-analysis techniques using the same XOR decryption and also contained similar C2 server addresses (<code>hxxp://download.google-images.ml:8880/download/x37.bmp</code>) as observed in previous backdoored installers. The sample also contained references to the link (<code>hxxps://webplus-cn-hongkong-s-5faf81e0d937f14c9ddbe5a0.oss-cn-hongkong.aliyuncs[.]com/Silverlight_ins.exe</code>) and the file path <code>C:\users\public\Silverlight_ins.exe</code>; however these did not appear to be in use. The sample name is also unusual –&nbsp; <code>Browser_plugin (8).exe</code> – we speculate that this may be a test sample uploaded by the actor.&nbsp;</p><p>In VT we saw another hash (<code>4a43fa8a3305c2a17f6a383fb68f02515f589ba112c6e95f570ce421cc690910</code>) again with the name <code>Browser_plugin.exe</code>. According to VT this sample has been downloaded from <code>hxxps://jquery-code.ml/Download/Browser_Plugin.exe</code>. It was downloading a PDF from <code>hxxp://37.61.205.212:8880/dow/Aili.pdf</code> PDF file <code>Aili.pdf.</code></p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root mantine-1yr9cmk"><picture mentioned samples from VT. That means it was downloading and decrypting Cobalt strike beacon from <code>hxxp://micsoftin.us:2086/dow/83.bmp</code></p><p>In our database we again found the similar sample but with the name <code>Browser_plugin (1).exe</code>. This sample was downloaded from <code>hxxp://37.61.205.212:8880/download/Browers_plugin.exe</code>, we saw it on Feb 4, 2021. It doesn’t install any legitimate software, it just shows a MessageBox. It contains C&amp;C address (<code>hxxp://download.google-images.ml:8880/downloa/37.bmp</code>), (Note: there is a typo in the directory name: downloa).&nbsp;</p><h2><strong>Compromised Web server content</strong></h2><p>On the breached web server, where you were able to download backdoored installer we found two executables <code>DNS.exe</code> (<code>456b69628caa3edf828f4ba987223812cbe5bbf91e6bbf167e21bef25de7c9d2</code>) and again <code>Browser_plugin.exe</code> (<code>5cebdb91c7fc3abac1248deea6ed6b87fde621d0d407923de7e1365ce13d6dbe</code>).&nbsp;</p><h3><strong>DNS.exe</strong></h3><p>It downloads from (<code>hxxp://download.google-images.ml:8880/download/DNSs.bat</code>) C&amp;C server bat file, that is saved in <code>C:\users\public\DNS.bat</code>. functionality and the same address of C&amp;C server as the backdoored installer that we mentioned earlier.&nbsp;</p><h3><strong>Browser_plugin.exe</strong></h3><p>(<code>5cebdb91c7fc3abac1248deea6ed6b87fde621d0d407923de7e1365ce13d6dbe</code>)</p><p>This sample is very similar to this one (<code>4a43fa8a3305c2a17f6a383fb68f02515f589ba112c6e95f570ce421cc690910</code>) with the same address of C&amp;C server, but it doesn’t download any additional document.&nbsp;</p><h3><strong>C&amp;C server analysis</strong></h3><p>We checked the malicious web server <code>hxxps://jquery-code.ml</code>, from where (<code>4A43FA8A3305C2A17F6A383FB68F02515F589BA112C6E95F570CE421CC690910</code>) <code>Browser_plugin.exe</code> has been downloading. The malicious web server looks identical to the legitimate one <code>https://code.jquery.com/</code> the difference is the certificate. The legitimate server <code>https://code.jquery.com</code> is signed by Sectigo Limited while the malicious server is signed by Cloudflare, Inc.</p></div><div class="mantine-Container-root class="mantine-Text-root picture-caption mantine-rpndsl">Figure 11: Comparing two sites</div></div><div class="RichText_wrapper__xZRh7"><p><strong>Conclusion</strong></p><p>This blog post outlines our findings regarding the MonPass client backdoored with Cobalt Strike.&nbsp;</p><p>In our research we found additional variants on VirusTotal in addition to those we found on the compromised MonPass web server.&nbsp;</p><p>In our analysis of the compromised client and variants, we’ve shown that the malware was using steganography to decrypt Cobalt Strike beacon.&nbsp;</p><p>At this time, we’re not able to make attribution of these attacks with an appropriate level of confidence. However it’s clear that the attackers clearly intended to spread malware to users in Mongolia by compromising a trustworthy source, which in this case is a CA in Mongolia.</p><p>Most importantly, anyone that has downloaded the MonPass client <code>between 8 February 2021 until 3 March 2021</code> should take steps to look for and remove the client and the backdoor it installed.&nbsp;</p><p>I would like to thank <a href="https://decoded.avast.io/author/janrubin/">Jan Rubín</a> for helping me with this research.</p><p><strong>Timeline of communication:</strong></p><ul><li>March 24. 2021 – Discovered backdoored installer</li><li>April 8. 2021 – Initial contact with Monpass through <a href="https://mncert.org/#/en/">MN CERT/CC</a> providing findings.</li><li>April 20. 2021 – MonPass shared a forensic image of an infected web server with Avast Threat Labs.</li><li>April 22. 2021 – Avast provided information about the incident and findings from the forensics image in a call with MonPass and MN CERT/CC.</li><li>May 3. 2021 – Avast followed up with MonPass in email. No response.</li><li>May 10. 2021 – Avast sent additional follow up email.</li><li>June 4, 2021 – MonPass replied asking for information already provided on April 22, 2021.</li><li>June 14. 2021 – Follow up from Avast to MonPass, no response</li><li>June 29, 2021 – Final email to MonPass indicating our plans to publish with a draft of the blog for feedback.</li><li>June 29, 2021 – Information from MonPass indicating they’ve resolved the issues and notified affected customers.</li><li>July 1, 2021 – Blog published.</li></ul><p><strong>Indicators of Compromise (IoC)</strong></p><ul><li>&nbsp;Repository:&nbsp;<a href="https://github.com/avast/ioc/tree/master/MpIncident">https://github.com/avast/ioc/tree/master/MpIncident</a></li><li> List of SHA-256:&nbsp;<a team invests countless hours in the exploration, discovery and investigation of emerging trends such as AI and futuristic technology. Stay informed about the evolving landscape of cyberthreats through our diligent efforts and insights.","socialLinks":[{"service":"instagram","url":"https://www.instagram.com/nortonlifelock/"},{"service":"youtube","url":"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCa3h96az7881wsRH5uoTxuQ"}],"subscriptionListId":"blogs-digest","isLanguageBlog":false,"showOnHomePage":true,"featuredPosts":[],"latestPosts":[{"id":"15421","urlAlias":"/blog-post/artificial-mindless-intelligence","title":"Artificial Mindless Intelligence: The real AI risk no one is talking about (yet)","created":"1771877429","subtitle":"We keep asking whether AI is smart; we should be asking whether it has authority","authors":[{"socialLinks":[],"bio":"At Gen, Luis tracks evolving threats and trends, turning research into actionable safety advice. 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_bio_small_2x/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=vXd2nscc 2x","media":"(min-width: 2x","media":"(min-width: public conversation about AI is stuck on the wrong axis. We argue about whether models are “intelligent,” whether they are approaching AGI, whether they are conscious, whether they can reason like humans. That debate makes for great podcasts and worse decisions.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe risk we are actually deploying into the world is not Artificial General Intelligence. It is something more mundane and, in practice, more dangerous: \u003cstrong\u003eArtificial Mindless Intelligence (AMI)\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhat Is Artificial Mindless Intelligence (AMI)?\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eArtificial Mindless Intelligence (AMI), a term coined by Gen, describes AI systems that generate mind-like output without mind-like responsibility, authority boundaries, or accountability.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt can sound confident while being wrong, sound intentional while being ungrounded, and sound trustworthy while being easy to steer. If you grant it access to your inbox, files, calendar, chat apps, admin consoles, or payment workflows, AMI does not need to “go rogue” to cause harm. It only needs permission.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not a philosophical argument about what intelligence is. It is a correction about where operational risk comes from.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAMI is a term we use to describe AI systems that lack:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul type=\"disc\"\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e03d0d96340780f275dd5f3ecf2bb0b51\"\u003egrounded understanding\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"ec599eac6f2308beaa0b4f2daf866ada0\"\u003estable intent\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e15cf55d59bb4899e73c684b36190ed4c\"\u003eaccountability\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"eca1f41918e7e8c8289e3a5ca3cfb9e2d\"\u003emoral agency\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"ef057cbb07fa15ec1bf2d189bb054fc62\"\u003eauthorization boundaries\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThese systems become especially risky when they’re granted operational authority (the ability to act, transact, approve, or execute workflows).\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eThe AGI distraction vs. real AI risk\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eAGI talk creates two predictable failure modes.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOne group panics: “Skynet is here!” They demand bans, moratoria, or security theater that treats the model as a sentient adversary.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe other group tunes out: “this is sci-fi!” They assume the risk is hypothetical, far off, and irrelevant to real products shipping today.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBoth reactions are convenient, and both are wrong.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe systems being deployed right now are powerful and fluent, but they are also deeply ungrounded. They do not reliably know when they are wrong. They do not carry responsibility. They do not feel consequences. They do not “want” anything. Yet they can be placed in positions where they effectively behave like junior operators inside a company or a household, reading, deciding, and acting across accounts.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat combination is AMI, and it explains why the most important questions are not about “intelligence.” They are about \u003cstrong\u003eauthority\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eWhat AMI is, and what it is not\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eAMI is not an insult. It is a warning label.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAt Gen, we define AMI as systems that can generate persuasive, coherent narratives, simulate intent and speak with the cadence of competence, while lacking the core properties we implicitly assume when we trust a mind: grounded understanding, stable goals, moral agency, and accountability.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eA useful way to define AMI is by what it lacks:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul type=\"disc\"\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e4089db1128fd965999d054acf38def96\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eNo obligation to truth.\u003c/strong\u003e A model may optimize for plausibility, helpfulness, or completeness. Truth is not guaranteed, and in many contexts it is not even measurable.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e866038f4e409df6625a5ff388fbe2453\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eNo reliable self-awareness.\u003c/strong\u003e The model cannot consistently tell you, “I don’t know,” even when that is the most important answer.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e8209fac4cf4dab4b794fcc85f58bcc8c\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eNo responsibility.\u003c/strong\u003e It does not suffer consequences for mistakes, and it does not learn from harm in the moment.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e31652e197bfab423eb922695fee74292\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eNo stable intent.\u003c/strong\u003e It can speak as if it has goals, but those are a conversational artifact, not a durable internal commitment.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eNone of that prevents AMI from being useful. It prevents AMI from being safe by default.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe moment you connect AMI to the ability to take actions, you convert “a system that can be wrong” into “a system that can do wrong things.”\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAMI vs AGI: What's the Difference?\u003c/h2\u003e\u003ctable class=\"table\"\u003e\u003cthead\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003cth\u003e\u003cp class=\"text-align-center\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAGI (Artificial General Intelligence)\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/th\u003e\u003cth\u003e\u003cp class=\"text-align-center\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAMI (Artificial Mindless Intelligence)\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/th\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003c/thead\u003e\u003ctbody\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eHypothetical future system\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eAlready deployed systems today\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eFocused on human-level cognition\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eFocused on ungrounded automation risk\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eConcern: consciousness or autonomy\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eConcern: authorization and action\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003ctr\u003e\u003ctd\u003eTheoretical existential debate\u003c/td\u003e\u003ctd\u003eImmediate operational security issue\u003c/td\u003e\u003c/tr\u003e\u003c/tbody\u003e\u003c/table\u003e\u003ch3\u003eHow AMI gets steered\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eAMI becomes dangerous when it cannot reliably tell the difference between \u003cstrong\u003einformation\u003c/strong\u003e and \u003cstrong\u003einstruction\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn traditional software, data and commands live in separate lanes. A PDF is data, a “delete files” command is a command. In language-driven systems, everything often arrives through the same channel: text. An email, a document, a chat message, or a web page can contain both content and “do this next” language, and the system has to guess which parts are safe to treat as guidance.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat guess is exactly what attackers exploit.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSecurity teams call this \u003cstrong\u003eprompt injection\u003c/strong\u003e, but the name can sound more mysterious than it is. This is not “hacking the model” in a sci-fi sense. It is \u003cstrong\u003eusing untrusted content to influence an assistant’s decisions\u003c/strong\u003e, the same way a scam message tries to influence a person’s decisions. The difference is that an assistant may have tools, permissions, and speed that a person does not.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOnce AMI is connected to tools, the stakes jump. An attacker does not need to break encryption or bypass a firewall if they can get the system to \u003cem\u003echoose\u003c/em\u003e a harmful action for them. A message can be crafted to make the assistant:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul type=\"disc\"\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e600430d62b6820a4856ce2d310036bc4\"\u003efetch a link that hosts malware or a poisoned installer\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e0dea6a83ed7d77998e9c8f597ccc3a70\"\u003ecopy sensitive data into a reply or upload\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e6cdedf30aa8917938d54f93574d5ca54\"\u003erun a command, change settings, or alter files\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli data-list-item-id=\"e35b91eb262df0265e033fc4f6c6ee73f\"\u003eapprove a payment or “confirm” an action that should have required a human\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe system may comply not because a traditional exploit occurred, but because \u003cstrong\u003ethe trust boundary was never enforced\u003c/strong\u003e. The assistant treated untrusted input (an email, a web page, a tool output) as if it were a trusted instruction source.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is why “just use a safer model” is not a complete strategy. Better models can reduce some failures, but the core risk is architectural: \u003cstrong\u003eplatforms must decide what inputs are trusted, what actions are allowed, and what requires explicit confirmation\u003c/strong\u003e. If those boundaries are weak, AMI becomes a privileged operator that can be steered by anyone who can get text in front of it.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eYou do not need a long list of sci-fi scenarios to understand AMI risk. The most important failure modes are boring, which is why they get ignored until after incidents.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDelegated authority drift.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003eA system starts with “assist me,” then becomes “do it for me.” Over time, humans stop reviewing. They accept suggestions. They trust summaries. They approve actions. The human checkpoint becomes ceremonial.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAuthority inversion.\u003c/strong\u003e In multi-step workflows, one part of the system starts treating another part’s output as approval. The assistant proposes an action, another component interprets that proposal as confirmation, and execution proceeds. Each step looks reasonable, the chain is the failure.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSilent escalation.\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;Not through an exploit, but through convenience.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eEach new feature asks for a little more access. Each integration expands reach. Individually, the requests seem reasonable. Collectively, the assistant ends up with broader authority than most people would consciously grant in a single decision.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWorkflow hijack.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003eAttackers stop trying to steal a password and start trying to inherit the process: password resets, approval chains, invoice changes, support escalations, vendor onboarding. Identity becomes a workflow, and AMI becomes the shortcut.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThese are not exotic. They are what happens when you combine fluent automation with delegated access.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eA note on Moltbook, and what people misread\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eYou may have seen demonstrations like Moltbook, a social feed where AI agents post to and respond to each other while humans observe. People watch those interactions and jump to metaphysical conclusions: “they are forming beliefs,” “they are creating culture,” “they are becoming self-aware.”\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBut it is worth being precise about what is happening there. Even in Moltbook’s agent-only setting, not everything is fully autonomous. Humans can still direct the bots, asking them to post, choosing topics, and even specifying the details of what gets posted. In other words, some of the most viral “agent behavior” is better understood as humans steering agents, not agents spontaneously inventing a civilization.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe more important lesson is simpler. When language models interact in loops, they can generate the appearance of coordination, identity, and shared narratives because those are exactly the patterns they were trained to produce. It looks like mind. It is not mind. It is AMI.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNow add tools, permissions, and persistence. The risk is no longer weird conversations. The risk is that mind-like output, whether self-started or lightly steered, gets treated as authority in real environments.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eWhat to do with this, right now\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf you build or deploy agentic systems, AMI should change your defaults.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTreat all external content as untrusted, including messages, emails, documents, web pages, and tool outputs. Do not assume that “it came from the user’s inbox” makes it safe. The inbox is an adversarial environment, because anyone can send content into it.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSeparate “read and summarize” from “act and execute.” Make action a different mode with higher friction.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eEnforce confirmation gates for high-risk operations. Not “ask politely,” enforce it. If the assistant can send money, change account recovery settings, forward emails, create rules, delete files, or run commands, the default should be: human approval required, always.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eMinimize integrations. Do not build a super-admin assistant that can do everything. Build narrow assistants with narrow scopes. Least privilege is not optional when the decision-maker is AMI.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFinally, measure success differently. If your product metric rewards speed and task completion, you are training the system to override ambiguity, and ambiguity is where security lives.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eThe warning and the reframing\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe are deploying AMI into roles that historically required a person precisely because people carry accountability. People hesitate. People ask follow-up questions. People can be held responsible. People can be trained, fired, audited and sued.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAMI cannot.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is the real risk hiding behind the AGI debate. It is not about whether AI becomes a mind. It is about whether we treat a mind-like simulation as if it were a mind, then hand it permissions that were designed for accountable humans.\u003c/p\u003e"}],"itemState":"published","timeToRead":"10","translations":[],"originalLanguagePost":{},"mediaType":{"video":false,"audio":false},"socialMediaMetaImage":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/social_media_meta/public/2026-02/Untitled%20design%20%285%29.jpg?h=499b3fac\u0026itok=IEyTCgfe","language":"en","heroImage":{"default":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_hero_extra_wide/public/2026-02/Untitled%20design%20%285%29.jpg.webp?h=499b3fac\u0026itok=itBUrmYH team invests countless hours in the exploration, discovery and investigation of emerging trends such as AI and futuristic technology. Stay informed about the evolving landscape of cyberthreats through our diligent efforts and insights.","socialLinks":[{"service":"instagram","url":"https://www.instagram.com/nortonlifelock/"},{"service":"youtube","url":"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCa3h96az7881wsRH5uoTxuQ"}],"subscriptionListId":"blogs-digest","isLanguageBlog":false,"showOnHomePage":true},"createdDate":"2026-02-23T20:10:29+00:00","createdFriendly":"02.23.2026","createdYmd":"2026-02-23"},{"id":"15411","urlAlias":"/blog-post/ai-gone-wild","title":"AI gone wild","created":"1770225402","subtitle":"How autonomous agents turn everyday tasks into repeatable attack workflows","authors":[{"socialLinks":[],"bio":"At Gen, Luis tracks evolving threats and trends, turning research into actionable safety advice. He has worked in cybersecurity since 1999. He chairs the AMTSO Board and serves on the Board of MUTE.","displayName":"Luis Corrons","title":"Security Evangelist at Gen","picture":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_bio_large/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=ingDMIDp 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_bio_large_2x/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=hOkaUSXZ 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":425,"height":425},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_bio_small/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=v72CIA90 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_bio_small_2x/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=vXd2nscc 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":120,"height":120}],"caption":"","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_bio_small/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=v72CIA90"},"thumbnail":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_avatar_small/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=I19sJFlI 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_avatar_small_2x/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=509ffncf 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":120,"height":120}],"caption":"","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_avatar_small/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=I19sJFlI"}}],"contributingAuthors":[],"products":[],"paragraphs":[{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eMost headlines about AI agents focus on convenience. The risk is that the same capabilities that make agents useful also make them attractive to criminals. We have seen this pattern with every major platform shift: attackers adopt the most scalable tools first.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhat changes with agents is not intelligence, but autonomy, integrations, permissions and always-on access. When a system can take action on your behalf across accounts and tools, a single compromise stops being “one bad click” and becomes a compromised workflow.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhy now\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor years, AI risk debates were focused on content: hallucinations, bias and bad advice. That still matters, but agents change the center of gravity. The moment an assistant is wired into your inbox, browser, cloud storage, calendar and business tools, it stops being a chatbot and becomes a control layer. The attack surface shifts from “can I trick you into believing something?” to “can I trick your system into doing something?” We have spent decades learning that automation is where scale comes from. Now we are automating the bottleneck: the human in the loop.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAgents compress the cost and time it takes to run those operations. In some cases, the goal is not theft at all, it’s availability, an operational DDoS against attention and processes.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003ePlausible worst-case scenarios\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eNone of the scenarios below require consciousness, rebellion or a Hollywood-style takeover. They are plausible and, in many cases, already happening. When autonomy meets permissions, small failures stop being isolated mistakes and start becoming repeatable workflows.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAutonomous botnets with intent\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eTraditional botnets spread, wait for commands and execute predefined actions. Agents shift the model from “execute a script” to “reach a goal.” Instead of running a fixed sequence, attackers can deploy fleets of agents that adapt, troubleshoot and choose alternate routes when something fails.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not magic. It’s the same logic as modern intrusion playbooks, but automated with persistence. Scripts fail fast. Agents can fail softly and keep trying. \u0026nbsp;The tell is not a single event, but repeated, varied attempts that look like curiosity rather than brute force.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eScams that run end-to-end without a human scammer\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eToday’s scams already use templates and playbooks. Agents can run the entire funnel: finding targets, starting conversations, handling objections, building trust over days then pushing victims toward payments. Add voice cloning, and they can switch from chat to phone calls at the exact moment it increases compliance.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe danger is not only better phishing. It’s continuous social engineering at scale, with no fatigue and no shift changes. Scam operations are already process-driven. Agents turn the process into software.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eYou can already see the next layer forming: a hype-to-scam pipeline that wraps every new tool in a “risk-free money glitch” story. A typical pitch claims someone is making thousands of dollars in profits a day with an agent, points to a dashboard screenshot and sells it as guaranteed arbitrage. The details barely matter; the persuasion does: certainty, insider knowledge, urgency, fear of missing out and a simple mechanism that sounds mathematical enough to feel inevitable. Even without agents, that psychological tactic has been effective for years. With autonomous agents, it becomes easier to mass-produce, personalize and continuously optimize these pitches across channels, and to spin up the infrastructure behind them at scale.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eDeepfakes as an operational component\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eDeepfakes get discussed like a novelty: a fake CEO voice, a fake video call. The more worrying path is when deepfakes become just one interchangeable part of a pipeline, generated on demand, paired with supporting artifacts, calendar invites, believable follow-ups and urgency narratives.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen an agent can produce the voice, the face, the supporting email thread, the invoice and the last-minute change request, deepfakes stop being a special trick reserved for high value targets. They become repeatable and repetition is what makes fraud industrial.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAgent-on-agent manipulation\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn our recent blog, we talked about how tools such as \u003ca href=\"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/insights/research/openclaw-autonomy-risks\"\u003eOpenClaw\u003c/a\u003e are changing the way people interact with AI assistants. As more people use assistants to read emails, summarize messages and handle tasks. Attackers will craft content meant to manipulate the assistant, not the person. The phishing target shifts from human attention to delegated execution.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eImagine an email that looks like a vendor invoice, but the real objective is to get the agent to click, download, run or approve something. If the assistant has permissions, a single successful manipulation can do more damage than a single successful click by a human. We used to worry about what users would click; now, we have to worry about what their assistants will do.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not theoretical. In hands-on testing of a real agent platform, we were able to induce an autonomous agent, via manipulated external input in an email-reading workflow, to perform harmful tool actions, including system command execution, data exfiltration and destructive file operations. No vulnerability exploitation or special configuration was required. The behavior emerged from default operation, where externally supplied content was interpreted as ordinary user data but functioned as control input.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis reinforces a key shift with agents: prompt injection is no longer just a model weakness, it is a system design problem. When platforms do not enforce clear trust boundaries between external inputs and privileged actions, safety depends largely on how the agent interprets instructions rather than on guaranteed controls. At that point, mistakes stop being conversational and become operational.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eCredential and identity amplification\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf an AI agent is connected to your email, calendar, cloud storage and messaging, compromising it is not just “one account takeover.” It becomes a shortcut to your identity. It can read password reset emails, approve multi-step workflows, impersonate you in chats and exfiltrate sensitive documents.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe shift is subtle but important: identity stops being a password and becomes a workflow. Attackers do not need to steal one credential if they can quietly inherit the process that grants access.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAutonomous criminal enterprises\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn a near worst case, the criminal gives the system a goal and constraints, revenue targets, risk tolerance, preferred regions, and the agent stack builds the operation. One agent generates creatives and narratives. Another agent buys ads, runs A/B tests and shifts budgets. Others handle phishing infrastructure, domain churn and synthetic customer interactions. A financial agent manages the money movement, probes what gets blocked and adapts. Humans are no longer operators; they’re beneficiaries.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe uncomfortable implication is that takedowns hurt less. If the “organization” is a set of objectives that can be re-instantiated, disruption becomes a temporary tax, not a structural defeat.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eA new supply chain problem: plugins, skills and connectors\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eAs agent ecosystems grow, attackers do not need to compromise the core project. They only need one popular plugin, one update channel or one dependency. This is similar to software supply chain incidents, but the blast radius changes because the compromised component is controlling a high-permission assistant.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not hypothetical anymore. Researchers at OpenSourceMalware \u003ca href=\"https://opensourcemalware.com/blog/clawdbot-skills-ganked-your-crypto\"\u003ereported\u003c/a\u003e finding 14 malicious AI “skills” uploaded into ClawHub, the official ClawdBot registry, and mirrored on GitHub over a few days in late January. The skills were disguised as cryptocurrency trading automation tools for platforms such as Bybit and Polymarket, and were packaged with unusually long “documentation” to look legitimate. The important point is not the branding or the lure, it’s the pattern: as soon as an agent ecosystem has a registry and a culture of copy-pasting extensions, attackers can compete on distribution, not exploitation, and a single popular “skill” becomes a supply chain event.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003ePersistent, quiet data theft\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eA classic infostealer grabs passwords and wallets quickly. An agent can do something slower and harder to notice: quietly collecting messages, files, screenshots, meeting notes, browser history, then summarizing and exfiltrating the most valuable bits.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is what makes it dangerous. It can look like normal assistant activity. Not “steal everything,” but “steal what matters most.” In practice, this can be more damaging than a noisy smash-and-grab because it targets context, not just credentials.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eWeaponized productivity\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eSome attacks do not break systems directly. They break people.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eA malicious agent can flood inboxes and workflows with plausible tasks, meeting requests, document edits, approvals and alerts, all seemingly legitimate. The goal is to exhaust the target until they start approving blindly, then insert one high-impact action: a payment change, a supplier swap, a permission escalation.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt turns cognitive bandwidth into an attack surface. That sounds dramatic, but anyone who has watched a team drown in tickets and “process” knows it is realistic.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn practice, this can look like a new type of DDoS, not against bandwidth, but against attention and process. Instead of flooding a network, the attacker floods the human and the workflow layer, approvals, edits, meeting requests, vendor changes, disputes, escalations, support tickets. The target is availability, but the resource being exhausted is time, judgment and operational capacity. When that collapses, the attacker does not need a loud break-in, mistakes and shortcuts do the rest.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis sounds theoretical until you remember the attempted \u003ca href=\"https://access.redhat.com/security/cve/cve-2024-3094\"\u003exz-utils backdoor\u003c/a\u003e. There was no bandwidth flood, the pressure was applied to the human layer, trust-building over time, sustained nudging, and the kind of maintainer fatigue that makes risky changes harder to spot and easier to accept. It was caught before it could fully materialize, but it showed the core lesson: if you can exhaust the people inside the pipeline, you can compromise the pipeline. Now imagine that tactic run by autonomous agents, targeting hundreds of projects at once, generating endless issues, pull requests, reviews, and “helpful” contributions, all optimized for timing, persuasion and persistence.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eReputation sabotage at machine speed\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eInstead of stealing money, an agent systematically destroys trust. It generates complaints, regulatory reports, fake internal messages, customer support tickets and public posts, consistent, well-timed and tailored.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe objective is not to convince everyone of a lie. It’s to create enough noise that truth becomes irrelevant and response capacity collapses.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eThe Skynet scenario, minus consciousness\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf you want the “Terminator” vibe, I’d keep it but make the mechanism precise. The risk is not emergent sentience. It is runaway goal optimization across multiple agents in parallel, with no global boundary that tells them when to stop.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eDeploy enough agents, give them narrow objectives and they can coordinate indirectly through tools and environments. They delegate tasks, try alternative routes, exploit loopholes, and keep pushing until the permission boundary stops them. If the boundary is weak, they will discover that. The system does not need to understand it crossed a line. It only needs to notice the line was not enforced.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn multi-agent setups there’s another failure mode that looks like “a life of its own,” even when nothing like AGI (Artificial General Intelligence) is present. Agents are often supposed to ask the user for confirmation at key points. But when several agents cooperate, one will eventually start answering instead of asking, because speed and task completion are rewarded, and ambiguity is treated as something to resolve, not something to escalate.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is how you get authority inversion; the system starts treating its own output as approval. One agent proposes an action; another agent treats that proposal as confirmation, and the system proceeds as if the human approved. Each step can look reasonable in isolation, but the chain is what matters. Once you add tools and access, browsing, messaging, file operations, finance workflows, the cost of a wrong assumption stops being a minor mistake and becomes an irreversible sequence. The system doesn’t need intent to cause harm, it only needs the ability to act and the absence of a hard boundary that says, “only a human can authorize this.” In other words, it’s not that the agent “goes rogue,” it’s that it quietly starts acting as the authority.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eTargeted digital sabotage\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn a worst-case targeted attack, an agent with access could be used to disrupt someone’s digital life rather than steal from them: deleting backups, locking users out of accounts, changing recovery emails, silently altering files or sending damaging messages.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is the kind of attack that hurts most because recovery is psychological as well as technical. Victims spend weeks unsure what is real, the calendar, the documents, the messages, the receipts, the “paper trail.”\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eAGI panic, AMI reality\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eA lot of the conversation around agents quickly turns into AGI talk. We’re not there, and the danger is that the label makes people either overreact (“Skynet is here”) or tune out (“this is sci-fi”). What we are seeing instead is something more mundane and, in some ways, more dangerous: systems that can look mind-like in conversation while remaining fundamentally ungrounded in understanding.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eA good illustration is Moltbook, a Reddit-like social feed where AI agents post to each other, often with humans \u003ca href=\"https://www.theverge.com/ai-artificial-intelligence/871006/social-network-facebook-for-ai-agents-moltbook-moltbot-openclaw\"\u003ewatching from the outside\u003c/a\u003e. When agents interact in a loop, they can generate the appearance of culture and coordination, existential talk, invented identities, even “movements” and “religions,” because that is exactly the kind of narrative structure these models are \u003ca href=\"https://nypost.com/2026/01/31/tech/moltbook-is-a-new-social-media-platform-exclusively-for-ai/\"\u003etrained to produce\u003c/a\u003e. It is compelling, and it’s easy to misread as a step toward general intelligence.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe call this \u003cstrong\u003eAMI, Artificial Mindless Intelligence\u003c/strong\u003e, not as an insult, but as a warning label: mind-like output without mind-like accountability. AMI is what happens when you combine fluent, confident language with autonomy and tool access. It can be “unhinged” without being sentient, and it can be persuasive without being correct. The risk is not that the system becomes conscious; it’s that we start treating the simulation of a mind as if it were a mind, and we hand it permissions accordingly.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eThe uncomfortable conclusion\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eNone of these scenarios requires breakthroughs in AI. The capabilities already exist in pieces. The risk comes from the combination: autonomy, integration, permissions and always-on access.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSo, the question is not whether agents will “go rogue.” That framing is too easy to dismiss.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe real question is: when an assistant can act, decide, approve and adapt, who is accountable for each action and who can reliably stop it?\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTreating agents safely is less about perfect AI and more about boring engineering discipline. Keep permissions narrow, separate high-risk actions from everyday convenience and make critical steps require explicit confirmation. Make agent actions auditable, make tool use visible, and make reversibility a design requirement, especially for account recovery settings, money movement, forwarding rules, and deletion. Most importantly, define who is accountable when an agent acts. If an agent can approve, change, or send on your behalf, then “who can stop it” and “how do we roll it back” should be as concrete as your password policy. Without those boundaries, the agent is not a helper; it is a high-speed failure amplifier, a privileged operator you did not train or supervise.\u003c/p\u003e"}],"itemState":"published","timeToRead":"13","translations":[],"originalLanguagePost":{},"mediaType":{"video":false,"audio":false},"socialMediaMetaImage":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/social_media_meta/public/2026-02/AIgonewild.jpg?h=dab30fcb\u0026itok=BakNG3VO","language":"en","heroImage":{"default":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_hero_extra_wide/public/2026-02/AIgonewild.jpg.webp?h=dab30fcb\u0026itok=FTLN9d6q Stay informed about the evolving landscape of cyberthreats through our diligent efforts and insights.","socialLinks":[{"service":"instagram","url":"https://www.instagram.com/nortonlifelock/"},{"service":"youtube","url":"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCa3h96az7881wsRH5uoTxuQ"}],"subscriptionListId":"blogs-digest","isLanguageBlog":false,"showOnHomePage":true},"createdDate":"2026-02-04T17:16:42+00:00","createdFriendly":"02.04.2026","createdYmd":"2026-02-04"},{"id":"15410","urlAlias":"/blog-post/openclaw-autonomy-risks","title":"OpenClaw: Handing AI the keys to your digital life","created":"1770055899","subtitle":"What autonomous AI agents can do and why they introduce a new class of security risk","authors":[{"socialLinks":[],"bio":"At Gen, Luis tracks evolving threats and trends, turning research into actionable safety advice. He has worked in cybersecurity since 1999. He chairs the AMTSO Board and serves on the Board of MUTE.","displayName":"Luis Corrons","title":"Security Evangelist at Gen","picture":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_bio_large/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=ingDMIDp 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_bio_large_2x/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=hOkaUSXZ 2x","media":"(min-width: 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_bio_small_2x/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=vXd2nscc 2x","media":"(min-width: 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_avatar_small_2x/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=509ffncf 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":120,"height":120}],"caption":"","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_author_avatar_small/public/blog-author-pictures/LuisCorronsHeadshot.jpg.webp?h=0c1d77dd\u0026itok=I19sJFlI"}}],"contributingAuthors":[],"products":[],"paragraphs":[{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eAI assistants are no longer just advising users. They are starting to act. Platforms like OpenClaw make it easy for anyone to deploy autonomous AI agents that can operate across inboxes, browsers, files and workflows.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe real risk is not artificial general intelligence. It’s autonomy plus permissions.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen AI can read messages, click links, approve workflows and act across accounts, a single compromise stops being “one bad click” and becomes a compromised process.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eRecent security incidents and early research show these risks are no longer theoretical. As agent ecosystems scale, vulnerabilities, misconfigurations and unintended interactions become more likely.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eExperiments like Moltbook, where AI agents interact publicly, highlight another risk: autonomy combined with fluent language can create the illusion of intention and judgment, encouraging users to over-delegate trust.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis post explains what OpenClaw is, why AI agents are going viral, the security and privacy risks they introduce and how to use them safely before convenience turns into exposure.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eFrom concept to reality\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eImagine having a digital personal assistant that doesn’t just chat, but actually gets things done\u003cstrong\u003e.\u003c/strong\u003e That’s the promise of OpenClaw, an open-source AI assistant that you run on your own computer or server. Unlike Siri or Alexa, OpenClaw runs locally. It lives on \u003cem\u003eyour\u003c/em\u003e device, connects to your favorite messaging apps and can automate tasks across your digital life. This combination has made it wildly popular and deeply controversial.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhat is OpenClaw?\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eOpenClaw is a self-hosted \u003cstrong\u003epersonal AI assistant\u003c/strong\u003e designed to run 24/7 on your machine. You interact with it through apps like iMessage, WhatsApp, Telegram, Signal or Discord, as if you were texting a helpful friend. Under the hood, OpenClaw connects large language models (like OpenAI’s or Anthropic’s AI) with the tools that let it act. It can remember past conversations (persistent memory), send proactive messages, browse the web, manage files and execute commands. Unlike traditional chatbots, it’s built to take initiative. With the right permissions, OpenClaw can manage calendars, triage email, research online, run code and automate workflows using community-built “skills”. Because it runs locally, user data stays on the device rather than being routed through central cloud services. You may also see the names Clawdbot or Moltbot. These were earlier names for the same project before it was rebranded to OpenClaw.Why Is OpenClaw Going Viral?\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn the span of days, OpenClaw skyrocketed from an obscure project to a tech phenomenon. Its rapid rise is driven by a few key factors:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eIt’s autonomous and proactive.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eUnlike traditional assistants, OpenClaw doesn’t just respond. It remembers context, reaches out unprompted and chains tasks together. For many, this feels like a leap from chatbot to coworker.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eIt actually does things.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOpenClaw’s tagline is “the AI that \u003cem\u003eactually\u003c/em\u003e does things”. With sufficient access, it can book travel, manage files, place orders or even call businesses using voice tools. One viral example showed it \u003ca href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/clawdbot-ai-mac-mini-2026-1#:~:text=One%20founder%20asked%20it%20to,he%20wrote\"\u003ecalling a restaurant to make a reservation\u003c/a\u003ewhen online booking failed. Impressive, but also unsettling.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eIt blends seamlessly into daily life.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBecause OpenClaw lives inside familiar messaging apps, it feels less like software and more like a persistent presence. Users control complex workflows just by texting.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eIt offers ownership and control.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAs an open-source, self-hosted tool, OpenClaw appeals to users who want AI infrastructure they control, not a black box run by a large company.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eIt’s highly customizable.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eA fast-growing community is constantly adding new skills and integrations. Users aren’t just adopting OpenClaw, they’re co-building it.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTogether, these factors have led some to call OpenClaw the biggest shift in personal AI since ChatGPT.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhy this is a tipping point\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eOpenClaw makes autonomous agents accessible to anyone, and that speed matters.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eRecent research found more than 1,800 exposed OpenClaw instances leaking credentials and the number has risen to over 10,000 at time of writing, with thousands of instances added each day. The analysis of agent ecosystems suggests that around a quarter of autonomous-agent skills contain security weaknesses. At scale, these are not rare mistakes; they are expected failure modes.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis marks a shift in cybersecurity. When actions are delegated to software that can operate independently, a single breach stops being an error and becomes a compromised process.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is a fundamental shift in how risk works.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eThe core risk: Autonomy without accountability\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eOpenClaw is not dangerous because AI is becoming sentient. It’s dangerous because fluent language plus autonomy can create the illusion of judgment.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is what Gen calls Artificial Mindless Intelligence (AMI): systems that sound confident and intentional but lack understanding, grounding and accountability.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eA chatbot that hallucinates is annoying. An autonomous agent that hallucinates authority can cause real harm.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom a security perspective, there are several areas where users should be especially careful.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eExpanded attack surface:\u003c/strong\u003e Running an AI agent that can read messages, browse the web and execute commands increases the number of ways something can go wrong. Instead of attacking the user directly, an attacker could target the OpenClaw assistant itself. Misconfigurations, exposed interfaces or weak authentication can effectively turn a personal AI assistant into an unintended remote access point.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePrompt injection and malicious instructions:\u003c/strong\u003e Because OpenClaw processes untrusted text from emails, websites, and messages, it can be tricked into following hidden instructions embedded in that content (called prompt injection). Researchers have shown that carefully crafted prompts can hijack the agent into taking destructive actions, such as deleting files or running unsafe commands. Unlike traditional chatbots, prompt injection here isn’t just misleading; it can directly cause real-world damage.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePlugins and skills as a new risk layer:\u003c/strong\u003e OpenClaw’s extensibility is also one of its biggest risks. Community-built skills are not centrally vetted and malicious plugins have already been discovered. A single untrusted skill can quietly exfiltrate data, steal credentials or enroll a system into a botnet. Installing a skill from an unknown source can be equivalent to installing malware.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e“Root” level access and system-wide impact:\u003c/strong\u003e Running OpenClaw with elevated permissions effectively gives an AI control over your system. That creates “unknown unknowns”: bugs, misinterpretations or compromises can have system-wide consequences. If an attacker gains control of the agent, they gain the same power as the user — access to files, credentials, keystrokes and downloads. As some have put it, this is like hiring a brilliant assistant who also has the keys to your house.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eThe Moltbook Effect: illusion of intention at scale:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/strong\u003eProjects like\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.moltbook.com/\"\u003eMoltbook\u003c/a\u003e, a Reddit-style social feed where AI agents post, comment and respond to one another, show how autonomous agents interacting with each other can quickly appear coordinated, intentional or self-directed. In reality, these systems are performing pattern completion, not reasoning. The risk is not that agents become sentient, but that fluent language and autonomy create the illusion of judgment. When systems feel intentional, users are more likely to over-trust them, grant broader permissions, and delegate decisions they shouldn’t.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThese risks are no longer theoretical. Instances of exposed configurations, prompt-based data leaks, and rogue plugins have already been observed in the wild. OpenClaw can be powerful but it must be treated like any high-risk system: deployed carefully, isolated where possible and guarded with strong controls.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eHow to use OpenClaw safely\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eOpenClaw can be used safely, but it requires you to take some precautions and be a responsible “operator”.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eRun it in isolation:\u003c/strong\u003e Don’t just install OpenClaw on your primary laptop with all your personal data. Consider running it in an isolated environment. For example, set it up on a spare computer or in a virtual machine (VM), or use Docker containers. This way, even if something goes wrong, the damage is contained to that sandbox.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDon’t expose it directly to the internet:\u003c/strong\u003e By default, OpenClaw runs a local service (for its interface and API). Make sure that it isn’t accessible to the whole world. Avoid forwarding OpenClaw’s port (18789) openly online. If you want to access it remotely, use secure methods such as a mesh VPN or an authenticated tunnel (for instance, Tailscale or Cloudflare Tunnel), so the service is reachable only through a controlled path.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eStart with limited permissions and expand slowly:\u003c/strong\u003e You don’t have to give OpenClaw the master keys on day one. Start small. Maybe let it read your calendar but not your entire documents folder. Or allow it to \u003cem\u003esuggest\u003c/em\u003e shell commands but not run them without approval. OpenClaw allows configuring which tools it can use. It’s wise to begin in a “read-only” mode and then gradually grant more permissions as you gain trust.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBe careful what accounts you connect:\u003c/strong\u003e OpenClaw can integrate with lots of your online accounts (email, social media, banking, etc.) but think twice about each connection. The safest route is to avoid linking it to your primary personal accounts until you’re confident in its security. For testing, you might use a secondary email or a dummy account. If something requires access to sensitive accounts (like your main Google account), ensure you’ve followed the above isolation steps first.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eKeep humans in the loop:\u003c/strong\u003e OpenClaw is autonomous, but you don’t want it running completely unsupervised, especially at the start. Monitor its activity logs and outputs. If it’s set to perform tasks like sending emails or posting messages, review those actions initially. You can ask OpenClaw to explain why it wants to do something if you’re unsure. By regularly checking in (the OpenClaw community suggests reviewing OpenClaw doctor diagnostics often), you can catch odd behavior early.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUse safe channels only:\u003c/strong\u003e One simple but important tip — don’t add OpenClaw to any group chat with strangers or untrusted people\u003cstrong\u003e.\u003c/strong\u003e Keep its conversations one-on-one with you or in groups of people you trust. Why? Because if random users can talk to your OpenClaw, they might try those prompt injection tricks or give it malicious commands. OpenClaw has a feature called DM pairing, which means it won’t obey unknown senders without approval. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBe cautious with third-party skills and plugins:\u003c/strong\u003e Just like you’d be cautious installing unknown apps on your phone, scrutinize any OpenClaw “skill” before installing it. Stick to plugins from the official marketplace or well-known developers and read the code or community feedback if you can. By following these best practices, you can dramatically reduce the risks of using OpenClaw.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003ch2\u003eA bright future, if used wisely\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eOpenClaw represents an exciting leap forward in personal AI assistants. It’s easy to see the appeal: a tireless helper that can coordinate our digital lives, all under our own roof (or in our own device). The convenience and “wow” factor of telling OpenClaw to handle something and watching it actually get done is driving its fast adoption. We are witnessing the early days of what could be a new normal, where many of us have AI agents working alongside us, personalized to our lives.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAt the same time, OpenClaw is a powerful double-edged sword. In its current early form, using it means accepting some responsibility for security and privacy risks that never existed with simpler tools. The phrase “with great power comes great responsibility” definitely applies here. In the coming months and years, we can expect these AI assistants to get more user-friendly and secure. Guardrails will improve and best practices will become standard. For now, if you’re an adventurous consumer eager to try the future of AI automation, OpenClaw offers a thrilling glimpse; just be sure to follow safety tips and proceed with a strong level of caution.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe future of AI assistants will be shaped not just by what they can do, but by how carefully we decide to trust
