a 23-day period, Gen Threat Labs analyzed 14.5 million ads running on Meta platforms across the EU and UK, representing more than 10.7 billion impressions. Nearly one in three of those ads (30.99%) pointed to a scam, phishing or malware link. In total, scam ads generated more than 300 million impressions in less than a month. The activity was highly concentrated, with just 10 advertisers responsible for over 56% of all observed scam ads. Repeated campaign clusters were traced to shared payment and infrastructure linked to China and Hong Kong, indicating organized, industrial-scale operations rather than isolated bad actors. Read on to learn about how this ecosystem operates, why existing enforcement fails to contain it and what the data reveals about who is driving scam advertising at scale.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAdvertising built to persuade\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eToday, social advertising is being used to defraud at scale across some of the largest platforms.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not about isolated policy failures or a handful of malicious advertisers slipping through moderation. It is about an advertising system that is structurally attractive to criminals and consistently delivers results for them.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAcross the internet, ads have quietly become one of the most efficient delivery mechanisms for scams, phishing and malware. Today, dangerous ads don’t look suspicious; they look professional, familiar and seem to target your exact needs. On social networks, the same optimization engines designed to maximize engagement and conversion are being repurposed to maximize victimization. And that’s not accidental.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eWhen advertising becomes the attack channel\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eMost people still picture cyberattacks starting with a sketchy download or a suspicious email or text. Increasingly, they start with something far more normal: an ad. Malvertising (malicious advertising) has surged because it gives criminals what every marketer wants: instant reach, precise targeting and scale. Gen telemetry shows that malvertising has become the single largest threat to individuals, accounting for \u003ca href=\"https://newsroom.gendigital.com/2026-01-20-Gen-Q4-Threat-Report-Shows-Scams-Thriving-Inside-Ads%2C-Feeds%2C-and-Video\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e41% of all cyberattacks\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eMalvertising has become the top threat to consumers because they don’t look like they used to. People think they still look like loud pop-ups that scream, “I’m a virus,” or fake subscriptions. The modern model is quieter and more effective. It is a social engineering toolkit that blends into whatever people already trust and are currently paying attention to. We see this clearly in \u003ca href=\"https://us.norton.com/blog/emerging-threats/threat-report-q3-2024\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e“scam-yourself” attacks\u003c/a\u003e like FakeCaptcha and ClickFix, where victims are nudged into doing the attacker’s job for them, approving a browser prompt, enabling push notifications, copying and pasting commands or “verifying” something that feels routine. Browser push notifications in particular have become a reliable hook, as one click can turn a normal website visit into a persistent stream of scam prompts and redirects.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAttackers also borrow directly from legitimate marketing. They ride trends, exploit urgency, and add credibility signals. That includes pairing malicious ads with deepfakes and timely themes, especially in investment and crypto scams. Gen researchers have \u003ca href=\"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/insights/reports/threat-report-q1-2025\" target=\"_blank\"\u003edocumented this extensively in CryptoCore campaigns\u003c/a\u003e, where deepfake videos and hijacked accounts were used to promote fraudulent investment schemes at scale.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAnother persistent and successful tactic is impersonation. Cybercriminals buy ads on major ad networks (including search ads) to pose as trusted brands. The outcomes range from malicious redirects to phishing sites to drive-by downloads where malware is installed as part of the ad’s click path, often without the victim realizing what happened until later.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAnd malvertising is not confined to shady corners of the web. Even the most trusted websites can unknowingly display malicious ads because ads are delivered through complex, automated supply chains. The net effect is that malicious advertising has become a fast, efficient way to scam and phish people. Gen research on social media platforms shows that malicious advertising comprises roughly \u003ca href=\"https://us.norton.com/blog/malware/malvertising\" target=\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e30 % of scam incidents observed across social networks\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e, making it one of the most common threats users encounter in feeds and ads.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf malvertising is now one of the primary ways criminals reach victims, then measuring what is happening inside major ad ecosystems is not just interesting; it is necessary.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eWhen online fraud metastasizes\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhat we’ve discovered is not just growth, it’s an uncontrolled spread. What starts as an isolated scam tucked into an obscure corner of the web does not stay there. It spreads, mutates, and embeds itself into channels billions of people use every day. Online advertising, once a tool to connect consumers with products and services, has become part of the attack surface itself.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eMeta’s family of apps, including Facebook, Instagram, Threads, WhatsApp and Messenger, reaches an astonishing number of people. In Q2 2025, Meta reported that \u003cem\u003edaily\u003c/em\u003e active users across its platforms reached \u003cstrong\u003eabout 3.48 billion \u003c/strong\u003eworldwide. When a system that large becomes a gateway for scams, the impact is no longer isolated.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eScam infrastructure does not operate in parallel to these platforms. It operates through them, leveraging their trust signals, engagement mechanics and targeting capabilities to propagate at speed.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is the context for what we measured.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eHow we measured the problem\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eOur Gen Threat Labs knew Meta ads were a problem and set out to measure the exact scale. Rather than chasing individual scam examples, we built a large-scale measurement pipeline around Meta’s Ad Transparency API, which provides visibility into ads that are currently active or were recently active on Meta platforms. Each day, we collected ads that were active or had recently been active, contained English ad text, and were delivered to users in regions covered by Meta’s Ad Transparency Library, primarily the EU and UK. While the ads themselves often promoted content intended for a global audience, our analysis focused on ads visible through the transparency framework rather than attempting to infer targeting outside of what the API explicitly exposes. We intentionally avoided narrowing the scope further in order to capture how scam advertising behaves at scale, not just in obvious edge cases.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis work exists because regulation forced visibility. In the EU and UK, ad transparency requirements expose advertiser behavior in ways that cannot be replicated elsewhere, enabling independent measurement of scam advertising at an industrial scale. Where such regulation is absent, scam activity does not disappear; it simply becomes harder to measure. Transparency does not create abuse; it reveals it.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor this analysis, we focused on consumer-facing ads and did not intentionally collect or analyze political advertising. Political ads are self-declared by advertisers, so while some may still appear in the dataset due to misclassification, they were not the target of this study.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eEven with these constraints, the dataset quickly became massive.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003e14.5 million ads, billions of impressions, one pattern\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eOver a 23-day period, we collected \u003cstrong\u003e14.57 million ads\u003c/strong\u003e, representing \u003cstrong\u003e10.76 billion impressions\u003c/strong\u003e delivered across the EU and UK. Even as a partial view of the ecosystem, the scale was impossible to ignore.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eUsing intelligence and classification technology, we identified ads pointing to infrastructure associated with e-commerce scams, phishing campaigns, malware distribution and other consumer-facing threats. This allowed us to move beyond ad text and examine what users were actually being sent to.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe results were not subtle. \u003cstrong\u003e4.51 million ads\u003c/strong\u003e in our dataset were identified as scam-related, meaning \u003cstrong\u003enearly one in three ads (30.99%)\u003c/strong\u003e pointed to scam infrastructure. In total, these scam ads generated \u003cstrong\u003e143.8 million impressions in the EU\u003c/strong\u003e and \u003cstrong\u003e304.11 million impressions across the EU and UK\u003c/strong\u003e in less than a month.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not an edge case. It is an\u003cstrong\u003e \u003c/strong\u003eincredible volume.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2026-02/Metascam1.jpg.webp?itok=iAC2JIBG 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":807},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2026-02/Metascam1.jpg.webp?itok=YN22y3R8 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":540}],"caption":"","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2026-02/Metascam1.jpg.webp?itok=YN22y3R8","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003ch3\u003eWhy takedowns don’t scale\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eVolume alone does not explain why this problem persists. So, we looked at behavior over time.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAcross the dataset, we repeatedly observed scammers reusing the same infrastructure, identical domains, and near-identical ad text across many campaigns. In multiple cases, when one ad disappeared from the ad library for policy violations, other ads using the \u003cstrong\u003esame domain and messaging\u003c/strong\u003e remained active until their campaigns naturally expired.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom the outside, it seems enforcement of scam ads is reactive. Ads appear to be removed one at a time, often following reports or reviews, while related campaigns using the same infrastructure continue running. Even when scam components are reused at scale, generalization appears slow, even if they are delivered by the same advertiser.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe cannot see Meta’s internal detection signals, so we are careful not to assign intent. But the observable outcome is clear: \u003cstrong\u003eknown scam building blocks often remain usable long after one instance is taken down.\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAt this scale, that distinction matters.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image_inline","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_medium/public/2026-02/Metascamads2.jpg.webp?itok=KEUgAnaC 1x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":1076,"height":807},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2026-02/Metascamads2.jpg.webp?itok=bXWYWgGB 1x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":720,"height":540}],"caption":"","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_inline_small/public/2026-02/Metascamads2.jpg.webp?itok=bXWYWgGB","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003ch3\u003eA small number of advertisers cause outsized harm\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eScale tells us how big the problem is. Enforcement shows us why it persists.\u003cbr\u003eBut, to understand who is actually driving the harm, we asked a different question: are scam ads driven by countless small actors or by a smaller number of highly active criminal groups?\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe answer was clear.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eScam advertising was also highly concentrated. The \u003cstrong\u003etop 10 scam advertisers alone accounted for 56.1% of all scam ads\u003c/strong\u003e, representing \u003cstrong\u003e2.53 million unique scam advertisements\u003c/strong\u003e and \u003cstrong\u003e57.92 million impressions\u003c/strong\u003e. A relatively small number of advertiser entities were responsible for a majority of observed harm.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThese advertisers are not anonymous hobbyists. They are organized, persistent operators running industrial-scale campaigns.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe repeatedly traced clusters of campaigns back to payers and infrastructure linked to China and Hong Kong, operating fleets of short-lived pages created almost exclusively to run ads. Western brand names and English storefronts cycled rapidly, while payers and beneficiaries rotated or disappeared from disclosures altogether. What did not rotate were the mechanics: the same domains, URL patterns and ad behaviors resurfaced across supposedly unrelated advertisers.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eWhat comes next\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe numbers alone are unsettling. Millions of scam ads. Hundreds of millions of impressions. A small number of actors are driving disproportionate harm, repeatedly and at a scale.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis isn’t about imperfect moderation. It’s about a system that, in practice, allows attackers to move faster than constraints can keep up. In our next blog post, we’ll move this research from measurement to mechanics. We break down the techniques scammers use to hide malicious ads in plain sight, including how they manipulate displayed URLs, chain redirects, blend malicious links with legitimate ones and exploit the limits of transparency tools themselves.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBecause understanding the size of the problem is only the first step. Understanding how it persists is what allows it to be this.\u0026nbsp;\u003cbr\u003e\u003cbr\u003eYou are in a regular work call on Teams, Zoom or Google Meet. Someone clicks a button at the start, a little icon shows up saying “Recording and transcription on”, and everyone ignores it. You go through the agenda, say goodbye and most people leave.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThen someone says the classic, “Can a couple of you stay a minute to talk about next steps?”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is usually when the real talk happens:\u0026nbsp;Who is really behind on a project, which customer might leave, where you will have to cut budget, who is getting promoted and who is not.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eNow the twist: in many tools, the AI transcript keeps running until the very last person leaves. Which means all that “after the meeting” talk is written down, and often shared with everyone who can see the meeting recap, including people who were not there at that moment.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eConvenient? Sure. Risky? Very.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis is not just a big enterprise problem. For small and mid-sized businesses, one careless transcript can expose more than any single email.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhat auto transcripts actually do\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eModern video tools love giving you a “smart recap” of your calls:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eA full transcript of everything that was said \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAutomatic notes, action items and highlights \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe option to read a meeting you never attended\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom a user's point of view, this feels like magic. If you join late, you can scroll up and see what you missed. If you skip a call, you can read the summary instead of watching a one-hour recording.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBut there are a few details most people forget:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe transcript usually covers the whole call, not just the time you were in the room \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIt often ends up stored in a shared space that more people can access than you think \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eIt is searchable text, very easy to copy, paste, forward and leak\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn short, that little “AI notes on” label means there is a silent participant that never forgets anything.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhy this is a big deal for small businesses\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf you run a small business, your meetings are often where the real strategy happens:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003ePricing decisions \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eSalary conversations \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCustomer problems \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eSupplier issues \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCash flow stress\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn a big corporation, there are policies, legal teams and IT departments that, hopefully, think about what gets recorded and for how long. In a small company, you might just click “try our new AI notes feature” and move on.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat is where the danger starts. A few examples:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe finance call, where you talk openly about which roles could be cut if revenue drops, then the transcript is auto-shared with the whole team. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe customer call where you discuss your real discount limits after the client leaves, and that text ends up in the same thread you share back with them. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe incident response call, where you list all the weak points in your network, and that transcript lives in a shared drive that is poorly protected.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eNone of this requires a hacker genius. It just needs one person to forward the wrong link, a shared space to be misconfigured, or an account to be compromised.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eThe invisible audience problem\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eMost people look only at the faces in the meeting grid and think, “This is my audience”.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWith auto transcription, the real audience is bigger:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eEveryone who can see the meeting recap or transcript later \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAny AI assistant that uses those transcripts to generate notes \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAnyone who gets access to your collaboration tool if their account is compromised\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eOn top of that, transcripts do not show tone of voice, irony or frustration. A tired joke like “if this deal does not land we will have to fire half the team, haha” can look very different in a transcript taken out of context.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf you would not be happy seeing a sentence printed on a slide in front of the whole company, it should probably not live forever in a meeting transcript either.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eSo, what can you do, practically?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eHere is a simple way to think about it that works both for employees and for small business owners.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e1. As an employee: treat the icon as a big red “Recorder on” light\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf the call says recording or transcription is on, assume:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eEverything you say can be read later by people who are not on the call now \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eYour words become searchable text that someone can forward or screenshot\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eTwo simple habits:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eBefore a sensitive side conversation, check if transcription is still on. If it is, ask the host to stop it or start a new call. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eDo not say in a transcribed call what you would never write in a work email.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt is not about being paranoid; it is about not being surprised later.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e2. As a small business owner or manager\u003c/strong\u003e,\u003cstrong\u003e set a few clear rules\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eYou do not need a 20-page policy. Start with something very basic:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMeetings you never transcribe\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003cbr\u003eFor example, performance reviews, HR issues, layoffs, legal topics and security incidents. \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMeetings where transcription is OK, but with care\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003cbr\u003eTeam catch-ups, planning calls, customer calls. Here, agree that: \u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe host announces at the start that transcription is on \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe host stops it before any “only for a few of us” discussion starts \u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMeetings where transcription is actually helpful and low risk\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003cbr\u003eTraining, webinars and large briefings where the goal is to share information widely.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eWrite this in a short internal note or wiki page. People cannot follow rules they have never seen.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e3. Control where transcripts live and who can see them\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAsk your IT person or your most technical colleague to check:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eWhere are meeting transcripts stored by default? \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eWho can access them by default? Only participants, the whole team, “anyone with the link”? \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eAre there settings to limit how long they are kept?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eEven just reducing “everyone forever” to “only participants for 90 days” makes a big difference.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003e4. Be careful with “AI notetaker” bots\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSome tools let you invite a bot that joins your call to take notes. It looks cute, but it is also another account that can access your meetings and data.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBefore you allow them:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eCheck which company runs that bot \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCheck their privacy policy and whether they use your data to train their models \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eDecide which types of meetings the bot is allowed to attend\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf you would not invite a stranger to sit in on a sensitive meeting, do not invite a bot either.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eMake a conscious choice, not an accidental one\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAI transcripts are not evil. They help people who are hard of hearing, they help non native speakers, they make note-taking easier and they help people catch up on meetings they could not attend.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe problem is not the technology; it is autopilot.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIf you are going to let an AI assistant listen to everything your company says on calls, do it with your eyes open:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eDecide which meetings it can listen to \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eDecide when it needs to stop listening \u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eDecide who is allowed to read what is written\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eOtherwise, you might wake up one day and realize that the most honest, unfiltered moments in your business, the “stay a minute after the call” moments, have been quietly written down and shared much more widely than you ever intended.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"}],"itemState":"published","timeToRead":"7","translations":[],"originalLanguagePost":{},"mediaType":{"video":false,"audio":false},"socialMediaMetaImage":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/social_media_meta/public/2026-01/aimeeting.jpg?h=c5fb2b27\u0026itok=1UJEwIdi","language":"en","heroImage":{"default":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_hero_extra_wide/public/2026-01/aimeeting.jpg.webp?h=c5fb2b27\u0026itok=9mgkvLfK
